<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the biological macromolecule. <p><b>Ribonucleic acid</b> (<b>RNA</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> molecule essential in various biological roles in <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">coding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">decoding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_RNA" title="Regulatory RNA">regulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>. RNA and deoxyribonucleic acid (<a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>) are <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>. Along with <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, nucleic acids constitute one of the four major <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> essential for all known forms of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. Like DNA, RNA is assembled as a chain of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, but unlike DNA, RNA is found in nature as a single strand folded onto itself, rather than a paired double strand. Cellular organisms use <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> (<i><b>mRNA</b></i>) to convey genetic information (using the <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nitrogenous bases</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a>, denoted by the letters G, U, A, and C) that directs synthesis of specific proteins. Many <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> encode their genetic information using an RNA <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>.
</p><p>Some RNA molecules play an active role within cells by catalyzing biological reactions, controlling <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>, or sensing and communicating responses to cellular signals. One of these active processes is <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein synthesis</a>, a universal function in which RNA molecules direct the synthesis of proteins on <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>. This process uses <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> (<i><b>tRNA</b></i>) molecules to deliver <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> (<i><b>rRNA</b></i>) then links amino acids together to form coded proteins.
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Regulatory_RNA"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Regulatory RNA</span></a>
<ul>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#In_RNA_processing"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">In RNA processing</span></a></li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_DNA">Comparison with DNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Comparison with DNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2126" data-file-width="2265" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png/220px-50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png/330px-50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png/440px-50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:50S-subunit_of_the_ribosome_3CC2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three-dimensional representation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/50S" title="50S">50S</a> ribosomal subunit. Ribosomal RNA is in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ochre#Noun" title="wikt:ochre">ochre</a>, proteins in blue. The active site is a small segment of rRNA, indicated in red.</div></div></div>
<div style="overflow:hidden">
<p>The chemical structure of RNA is very similar to that of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, but differs in three primary ways:
</p>
<ul><li>Unlike double-stranded DNA, RNA is usually a single-stranded molecule (ssRNA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> in many of its biological roles and consists of much shorter chains of nucleotides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/RNA#Double-stranded_RNA" title="RNA">double-stranded RNA</a> (dsRNA) can form and (moreover) a single RNA molecule can, by complementary base pairing, form intrastrand double helixes, as in tRNA.</li>
<li>While the sugar-phosphate "backbone" of DNA contains <i><a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a></i>, RNA contains <i><a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a></i> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Ribose has a <a href="/wiki/Hydroxy_group" title="Hydroxy group">hydroxyl</a> group attached to the pentose ring in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_nomenclature" title="Nucleic acid nomenclature">2'</a> position, whereas deoxyribose does not. The hydroxyl groups in the ribose backbone make RNA more chemically <a href="/wiki/Lability" title="Lability">labile</a> than DNA by lowering the <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>.</li>
<li>The complementary base to <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> in DNA is <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>, whereas in RNA, it is <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, which is an <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">unmethylated</a> form of thymine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biochemistry_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biochemistry-4">[4]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div>
<p>Like DNA, most biologically active RNAs, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRNA" title="MRNA">mRNA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNA" title="TRNA">tRNA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RRNA" title="RRNA">rRNA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SnRNA" title="SnRNA">snRNAs</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Non-coding_RNA" title="Non-coding RNA">non-coding RNAs</a>, contain self-complementary sequences that allow parts of the RNA to fold<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and pair with itself to form double helices. Analysis of these RNAs has revealed that they are highly structured. Unlike DNA, their structures do not consist of long double helices, but rather collections of short helices packed together into structures akin to proteins.
</p><p>In this fashion, RNAs can achieve chemical <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> (like enzymes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> For instance, determination of the structure of the ribosome—an RNA-protein complex that catalyzes peptide bond formation—revealed that its active site is composed entirely of RNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ribosome_activity_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ribosome_activity-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_structure" title="Nucleic acid structure">Nucleic acid structure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1010" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png/250px-Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png/375px-Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png/500px-Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piwi-siRNA-basepairing.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Watson-Crick base pairs in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SiRNA" title="SiRNA">siRNA</a> (hydrogen atoms are not shown)</div></div></div>
<p>Each <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotide</a> in RNA contains a <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a> sugar, with carbons numbered 1' through 5'. A base is attached to the 1' position, in general, <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> (A), <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> (C), <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> (G), or <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a> (U). Adenine and guanine are <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purines</a>, cytosine and uracil are <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidines</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> group is attached to the 3' position of one ribose and the 5' position of the next. The phosphate groups have a negative charge each, making RNA a charged molecule (polyanion). The bases form <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> between cytosine and guanine, between adenine and uracil and between guanine and uracil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15561141_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15561141-8">[8]</a></sup> However, other interactions are possible, such as a group of adenine bases binding to each other in a bulge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
or the GNRA <a href="/wiki/Tetraloop" title="Tetraloop">tetraloop</a> that has a guanine–adenine base-pair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15561141_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15561141-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RNA_chemical_structure.GIF"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="428" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/RNA_chemical_structure.GIF/220px-RNA_chemical_structure.GIF" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/RNA_chemical_structure.GIF/330px-RNA_chemical_structure.GIF 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/RNA_chemical_structure.GIF 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RNA_chemical_structure.GIF" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a fragment of an RNA, showing a guanosyl subunit.</div></div></div>
<p>An important structural component of RNA that distinguishes it from DNA is the presence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group at the 2' position of the <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose sugar</a>. The presence of this functional group causes the helix to mostly take the <a href="/wiki/A-DNA" title="A-DNA">A-form geometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> although in single strand dinucleotide contexts, RNA can rarely also adopt the B-form most commonly observed in DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>  The A-form geometry results in a very deep and narrow major groove and a shallow and wide minor groove.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> A second consequence of the presence of the 2'-hydroxyl group is that in conformationally flexible regions of an RNA molecule (that is, not involved in formation of a double helix), it can chemically attack the adjacent phosphodiester bond to cleave the backbone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="658" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG/280px-Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG/420px-Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG/560px-Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ciliate_telomerase_RNA.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">Secondary structure</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telomerase_RNA" title="Telomerase RNA">telomerase RNA</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>RNA is transcribed with only four bases (adenine, cytosine, guanine and uracil),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> but these bases and attached sugars can be modified in numerous ways as the RNAs mature. <a href="/wiki/Pseudouridine" title="Pseudouridine">Pseudouridine</a> (Ψ), in which the linkage between uracil and ribose is changed from a C–N bond to a C–C bond, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-methyluridine" title="5-methyluridine">ribothymidine</a> (T) are found in various places (the most notable ones being in the TΨC loop of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNA" title="TRNA">tRNA</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Another notable modified base is <a href="/wiki/Hypoxanthine" title="Hypoxanthine">hypoxanthine</a>, a deaminated adenine base whose <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleoside</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Inosine" title="Inosine">inosine</a> (I). Inosine plays a key role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wobble_hypothesis" title="Wobble hypothesis">wobble hypothesis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are more than 100 other naturally occurring modified nucleosides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The greatest structural diversity of modifications can be found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNA" title="TRNA">tRNA</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> while pseudouridine and nucleosides with <a href="/wiki/2%27-O-methylation" title="2'-O-methylation">2'-O-methylribose</a> often present in rRNA are the most common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The specific roles of many of these modifications in RNA are not fully understood. However, it is notable that, in ribosomal RNA, many of the post-transcriptional modifications occur in highly functional regions, such as the peptidyl transferase center <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and the subunit interface, implying that they are important for normal function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The functional form of single-stranded RNA molecules, just like proteins, frequently requires a specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_Tertiary_Structure" title="RNA Tertiary Structure">tertiary structure</a>. The scaffold for this structure is provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">secondary structural</a> elements that are hydrogen bonds within the molecule. This leads to several recognizable "domains" of secondary structure like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hairpin_loop" title="Hairpin loop">hairpin loops</a>, bulges, and <a href="/wiki/Internal_loop" title="Internal loop">internal loops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In order create, i.e., design, a RNA for any given secondary structure, two or three bases would not be enough, but four bases are enough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This is likely why nature has "chosen" a four base alphabet: less than four does not allow to create all structures, while more than four bases are  not necessary. Since RNA is charged, metal ions such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">Mg<sup>2+</sup></a> are needed to stabilise many secondary and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_Tertiary_Structure" title="RNA Tertiary Structure">tertiary structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomer</a> of RNA is <small>D</small>-RNA composed of <small>D</small>-ribonucleotides. All chirality centers are located in the <small>D</small>-ribose. By the use of <small>L</small>-ribose or rather <small>L</small>-ribonucleotides, <small>L</small>-RNA can be synthesized. <small>L</small>-RNA is much more stable against degradation by <a href="/wiki/Ribonuclease" title="Ribonuclease">RNase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25236655_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25236655-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other structured <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">biopolymers</a> such as proteins, one can define topology of a folded RNA molecule. This is often done based on arrangement of intra-chain contacts within a folded RNA, termed as <a href="/wiki/Circuit_topology" title="Circuit topology">circuit topology</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Synthesis of RNA is usually catalyzed by an enzyme—<a href="/wiki/RNA_polymerase" title="RNA polymerase">RNA polymerase</a>—using DNA as a template, a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a>. Initiation of transcription begins with the binding of the enzyme to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)">promoter</a> sequence in the DNA (usually found "upstream" of a gene). The DNA double helix is unwound by the <a href="/wiki/Helicase" title="Helicase">helicase</a> activity of the enzyme. The enzyme then progresses along the template strand in the 3’ to 5’ direction, synthesizing a complementary RNA molecule with elongation occurring in the 5’ to 3’ direction. The DNA sequence also dictates where termination of RNA synthesis will occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Primary_transcript" title="Primary transcript">Primary transcript</a> RNAs are often <a href="/wiki/Post-transcriptional_modification" title="Post-transcriptional modification">modified</a> by enzymes after transcription. For example, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poly(A)_tail" title="Poly(A) tail">poly(A) tail</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5%27_cap" title="5' cap">5' cap</a> are added to eukaryotic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-mRNA" title="Pre-mRNA">pre-mRNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> are removed by the <a href="/wiki/Spliceosome" title="Spliceosome">spliceosome</a>.
</p><p>There are also a number of <a href="/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA-dependent RNA polymerases</a> that use RNA as their template for synthesis of a new strand of RNA. For instance, a number of <a href="/wiki/Orthornavirae" title="Orthornavirae">RNA viruses</a> (such as poliovirus) use this type of enzyme to replicate their genetic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Also, RNA-dependent RNA polymerase is part of the <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> pathway in many organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_RNA">Types of RNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Types of RNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_RNAs" title="List of RNAs">List of RNAs</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="668" data-file-width="402" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png/170px-Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png/255px-Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png/340px-Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Full_length_hammerhead_ribozyme.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a <a href="/wiki/Hammerhead_ribozyme" title="Hammerhead ribozyme">hammerhead ribozyme</a>, a ribozyme that cuts RNA</div></div></div>
<p>Messenger RNA (mRNA) is the RNA that carries information from DNA to the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, the sites of protein synthesis (<a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a>) in the cell. The mRNA is a copy of DNA. The coding sequence of the mRNA determines the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> sequence in the <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> that is produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Cell_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cell-29">[29]</a></sup> However, many RNAs do not code for protein (about 97% of the transcriptional output is non-protein-coding in eukaryotes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>).
</p><p>These so-called <a href="/wiki/Non-coding_RNA" title="Non-coding RNA">non-coding RNAs</a> ("ncRNA") can be encoded by their own genes (RNA genes), but can also derive from mRNA <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transcriptome_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-transcriptome-34">[34]</a></sup> The most prominent examples of non-coding RNAs are <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> (tRNA) and <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> (rRNA), both of which are involved in the process of translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biochemistry_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biochemistry-4">[4]</a></sup> There are also non-coding RNAs involved in gene regulation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_processing" title="RNA processing">RNA processing</a> and other roles. Certain RNAs are able to <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyse</a> chemical reactions such as cutting and <a href="/wiki/Ligase" title="Ligase">ligating</a> other RNA molecules,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and the catalysis of <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bond</a> formation in the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ribosome_activity_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ribosome_activity-7">[7]</a></sup> these are known as <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_length">In length</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: In length">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the length of RNA chain, RNA includes <a href="/wiki/Small_RNA" title="Small RNA">small RNA</a> and long RNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noncoding_RNA_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noncoding_RNA-36">[36]</a></sup> Usually, <a href="/wiki/Small_RNA" title="Small RNA">small RNAs</a> are shorter than 200 <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nt</a> in length, and long RNAs are greater than 200 <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nt</a> long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lncRNA_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-lncRNA-37">[37]</a></sup> Long RNAs, also called large RNAs, mainly include <a href="/wiki/Long_non-coding_RNA" title="Long non-coding RNA">long non-coding RNA</a> (lncRNA) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRNA" title="MRNA">mRNA</a>. Small RNAs mainly include 5.8S <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> (rRNA), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5S_rRNA" title="5S rRNA">5S rRNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> (tRNA), <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNA</a> (miRNA), <a href="/wiki/Small_interfering_RNA" title="Small interfering RNA">small interfering RNA</a> (siRNA), <a href="/wiki/Small_nucleolar_RNA" title="Small nucleolar RNA">small nucleolar RNA</a> (snoRNAs), <a href="/wiki/Piwi-interacting_RNA" title="Piwi-interacting RNA">Piwi-interacting RNA</a> (piRNA), tRNA-derived small RNA (tsRNA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsRNA_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsRNA-38">[38]</a></sup> and small rDNA-derived RNA (srRNA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-srRNA_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-srRNA-39">[39]</a></sup>
There are certain exceptions as in the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5S_rRNA" title="5S rRNA">5S rRNA</a> of the members of the genus <a href="/wiki/Halococcus" title="Halococcus">Halococcus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>), which have an insertion, thus increasing its size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_translation">In translation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: In translation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">Messenger RNA</a> (mRNA) carries information about a protein sequence to the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>, the protein synthesis factories in the cell. It is <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">coded</a> so that every three nucleotides (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codon</a>) corresponds to one amino acid. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> cells, once precursor mRNA (pre-mRNA) has been transcribed from DNA, it is processed to mature mRNA. This removes its <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a>—non-coding sections of the pre-mRNA. The mRNA is then exported from the nucleus to the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>, where it is bound to ribosomes and <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translated</a> into its corresponding protein form with the help of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNA" title="TRNA">tRNA</a>. In prokaryotic cells, which do not have nucleus and cytoplasm compartments, mRNA can bind to ribosomes while it is being transcribed from DNA. After a certain amount of time, the message degrades into its component nucleotides with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Ribonuclease" title="Ribonuclease">ribonucleases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Cell_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cell-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">Transfer RNA</a> (tRNA) is a small RNA chain of about 80 <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> that transfers a specific amino acid to a growing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypeptide" title="Polypeptide">polypeptide</a> chain at the ribosomal site of protein synthesis during translation. It has sites for amino acid attachment and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticodon" title="Anticodon">anticodon</a> region for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codon</a> recognition that binds to a specific sequence on the messenger RNA chain through hydrogen bonding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transcriptome_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-transcriptome-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">Ribosomal RNA</a> (rRNA) is the catalytic component of the ribosomes. The rRNA is the component of the ribosome that hosts translation. Eukaryotic ribosomes contain four different rRNA molecules: 18S, 5.8S, 28S and 5S rRNA. Three of the rRNA molecules are synthesized in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleolus" title="Nucleolus">nucleolus</a>, and one is synthesized elsewhere. In the cytoplasm, ribosomal RNA and protein combine to form a nucleoprotein called a ribosome. The ribosome binds mRNA and carries out protein synthesis. Several ribosomes may be attached to a single mRNA at any time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Cell_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cell-29">[29]</a></sup> Nearly all the RNA found in a typical eukaryotic cell is rRNA.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TmRNA" title="TmRNA">Transfer-messenger RNA</a> (tmRNA) is found in many <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a>. It tags proteins encoded by mRNAs that lack stop codons for degradation and prevents the ribosome from stalling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulatory_RNA">Regulatory RNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Regulatory RNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The earliest known regulators of <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> were proteins known as <a href="/wiki/Repressor" title="Repressor">repressors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Activator_(genetics)" title="Activator (genetics)">activators</a> – regulators with specific short binding sites within <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_(genetics)" title="Enhancer (genetics)">enhancer</a> regions near the genes to be regulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>  Later studies have shown that RNAs also regulate genes. There are several kinds of RNA-dependent processes in eukaryotes regulating the expression of genes at various points, such as <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNAi</a> repressing genes <a href="/wiki/Post-transcriptional_regulation" title="Post-transcriptional regulation">post-transcriptionally</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Long_non-coding_RNA" title="Long non-coding RNA">long non-coding RNAs</a> shutting down blocks of <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">chromatin</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetically" title="Epigenetically">epigenetically</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_RNA" title="Enhancer RNA">enhancer RNAs</a> inducing increased gene expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Bacteria and archaea</a>  have also been shown to use regulatory RNA systems such as <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_small_RNA" title="Bacterial small RNA">bacterial small RNAs</a> and <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46">[46]</a></sup>  Fire and Mello were awarded the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> for discovering <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNAs</a>  (miRNAs), specific short RNA molecules that can base-pair with mRNAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="RNA_interference_by_miRNAs">RNA interference by miRNAs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: RNA interference by miRNAs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a></div>
<p>Post-transcriptional expression levels of many genes can be controlled by <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>, in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiRNA" title="MiRNA">miRNAs</a>, specific short RNA molecules, pair with mRNA regions and target them for degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>  This <a href="/wiki/Antisense_RNA" title="Antisense RNA">antisense</a>-based process involves steps that first process the RNA so that it can <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base-pair</a> with a region of its target mRNAs. Once the base pairing occurs, other proteins direct the mRNA to be destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nucleases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_non-coding_RNAs">Long non-coding RNAs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Long non-coding RNAs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Long_non-coding_RNA" title="Long non-coding RNA">Long Non-coding RNA</a></div>
<p>Next to be linked to regulation were <a href="/wiki/XIST" title="XIST">Xist</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_noncoding_RNA" title="Long noncoding RNA">long noncoding RNAs</a> associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X_chromosome_inactivation" title="X chromosome inactivation">X chromosome inactivation</a>.  Their roles, at first mysterious, were shown by <a href="/wiki/Jeannie_T._Lee" title="Jeannie T. Lee">Jeannie T. Lee</a> and others to be the <a href="/wiki/RNA_silencing" title="RNA silencing">silencing</a> of blocks of chromatin via recruitment of <a href="/wiki/Polycomb-group_proteins" title="Polycomb-group proteins">Polycomb</a> complex so that messenger RNA could not be transcribed from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Additional lncRNAs, currently defined as RNAs of more than 200 base pairs that do not appear to have coding potential,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup> have been found associated with regulation of <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluripotency" title="Pluripotency">pluripotency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhancer_RNAs">Enhancer RNAs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Enhancer RNAs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_RNA" title="Enhancer RNA">Enhancer RNA</a></div>
<p>The third major group of regulatory RNAs is called <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_RNA" title="Enhancer RNA">enhancer RNAs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup>  It is not clear at present whether they are a unique category of RNAs of various lengths or constitute a distinct subset of lncRNAs.  In any case, they are transcribed from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhancers" title="Enhancers">enhancers</a>, which are known regulatory sites in the DNA near genes they regulate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>  They up-regulate the transcription of the gene(s) under control of the enhancer from which they are transcribed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulatory_RNA_in_prokaryotes">Regulatory RNA in prokaryotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regulatory RNA in prokaryotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At first, regulatory RNA was thought to be a eukaryotic phenomenon, a part of the explanation for why so much more transcription in higher organisms was seen than had been predicted. But as soon as researchers began to look for possible RNA regulators in bacteria, they turned up there as well, termed as small RNA (sRNA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46">[46]</a></sup>  Currently, the ubiquitous nature of systems of RNA regulation of genes has been discussed as support for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_World" title="RNA World">RNA World</a> theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_small_RNA" title="Bacterial small RNA">Bacterial small RNAs</a> generally act via <a href="/wiki/Antisense_RNA" title="Antisense RNA">antisense</a> pairing with mRNA to down-regulate its translation, either by affecting stability or affecting cis-binding ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Riboswitch" title="Riboswitch">Riboswitches</a> have also been discovered. They are cis-acting regulatory RNA sequences acting <a href="/wiki/Allosteric_regulation" title="Allosteric regulation">allosterically</a>.  They change shape when they bind <a href="/wiki/Metabolite" title="Metabolite">metabolites</a> so that they gain or lose the ability to bind chromatin to regulate expression of genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaea also have systems of regulatory RNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>  The CRISPR system, recently being used to edit DNA <i>in situ</i>, acts via regulatory RNAs in archaea and bacteria to provide protection against virus invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_RNA_processing">In RNA processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In RNA processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="321" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg/220px-Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg/330px-Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg/440px-Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Synthesis_of_Pseudouridine.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uridine to pseudouridine is a common RNA modification.</div></div></div>
<p>Many RNAs are involved in modifying other RNAs.
<a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">Introns</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splicing_(genetics)" title="Splicing (genetics)">spliced</a> out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-mRNA" title="Pre-mRNA">pre-mRNA</a> by <a href="/wiki/Spliceosome" title="Spliceosome">spliceosomes</a>, which contain several <a href="/wiki/Small_nuclear_RNA" title="Small nuclear RNA">small nuclear RNAs</a> (snRNA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biochemistry_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Biochemistry-4">[4]</a></sup> or the introns can be ribozymes that are spliced by themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
RNA can also be altered by having its nucleotides modified to  nucleotides other than <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytidine" title="Cytidine">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanosine" title="Guanosine">G</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uridine" title="Uridine">U</a>.
In eukaryotes, modifications of RNA nucleotides are in general directed by <a href="/wiki/Small_nucleolar_RNA" title="Small nucleolar RNA">small nucleolar RNAs</a> (snoRNA; 60–300 nt),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transcriptome_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-transcriptome-34">[34]</a></sup> found in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleolus" title="Nucleolus">nucleolus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cajal_body" title="Cajal body">cajal bodies</a>. snoRNAs associate with enzymes and guide them to a spot on an RNA by basepairing to that RNA. These enzymes then perform the nucleotide modification. rRNAs and tRNAs are extensively modified, but snRNAs and mRNAs can also be the target of base modification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> RNA can also be methylated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="RNA_genomes">RNA genomes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: RNA genomes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like DNA, RNA can carry genetic information. <a href="/wiki/RNA_virus" title="RNA virus">RNA viruses</a> have <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a> composed of RNA that encodes a number of proteins. The viral genome is replicated by some of those proteins, while other proteins protect the genome as the virus particle moves to a new host cell. <a href="/wiki/Viroid" title="Viroid">Viroids</a> are another group of pathogens, but they consist only of RNA, do not encode any protein and are replicated by a host plant cell's polymerase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_reverse_transcription">In reverse transcription</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In reverse transcription">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Reverse transcribing viruses replicate their genomes by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription" title="Reverse transcription">reverse transcribing</a> DNA copies from their RNA; these DNA copies are then transcribed to new RNA. <a href="/wiki/Retrotransposon" title="Retrotransposon">Retrotransposons</a> also spread by copying DNA and RNA from one another,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Telomerase" title="Telomerase">telomerase</a> contains an <a href="/wiki/Telomerase_RNA_component" title="Telomerase RNA component">RNA that is used as template</a> for building the ends of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotic_chromosome_structure" title="Eukaryotic chromosome structure">eukaryotic chromosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Double-stranded_RNA">Double-stranded RNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Double-stranded RNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double-stranded_RNA.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="669" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Double-stranded_RNA.gif/220px-Double-stranded_RNA.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Double-stranded_RNA.gif/330px-Double-stranded_RNA.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Double-stranded_RNA.gif/440px-Double-stranded_RNA.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Double-stranded_RNA.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Double-stranded RNA</div></div></div>
<p>Double-stranded RNA (dsRNA) is RNA with two complementary strands, similar to the DNA found in all cells, but with the replacement of thymine by uracil and the adding of one oxygen atom. dsRNA forms the genetic material of some <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Double-stranded_RNA_viruses" title="Double-stranded RNA viruses">double-stranded RNA viruses</a>). Double-stranded RNA, such as viral RNA or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SiRNA" title="SiRNA">siRNA</a>, can trigger <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a> response in <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In Eukaryotes, Double-stranded RNA (dsRNA) plays a role in the activation of the innate immune system against viral infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circular_RNA">Circular RNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Circular RNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circular_RNA" title="Circular RNA">Circular RNA</a></div>
<p>In the late 1970s, it was shown that there is a single stranded covalently closed, i.e. circular form of RNA expressed throughout the animal and plant kingdom (see <a href="/wiki/Circular_RNA" title="Circular RNA">circRNA</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> circRNAs are thought to arise via a "back-splice" reaction where the <a href="/wiki/Spliceosome" title="Spliceosome">spliceosome</a> joins a upstream 3' acceptor to a downstream 5' donor splice site. So far the function of circRNAs is largely unknown, although for few examples a microRNA sponging activity has been demonstrated.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_discoveries_in_RNA_biology">Key discoveries in RNA biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Key discoveries in RNA biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_RNA_biology" title="History of RNA biology">History of RNA biology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:R_Holley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/R_Holley.jpg/210px-R_Holley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/R_Holley.jpg/315px-R_Holley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/R_Holley.jpg/420px-R_Holley.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:R_Holley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert W. Holley, left, poses with his research team.</div></div></div>
<p>Research on RNA has led to many important biological discoveries and numerous <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prizes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">Nucleic acids</a> were discovered in 1868 by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Miescher" title="Friedrich Miescher">Friedrich Miescher</a>, who called the material 'nuclein' since it was found in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> It was later discovered that prokaryotic cells, which do not have a nucleus, also contain nucleic acids. The role of RNA in protein synthesis was suspected already in 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Severo_Ochoa" title="Severo Ochoa">Severo Ochoa</a> won the 1959 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> (shared with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Kornberg" title="Arthur Kornberg">Arthur Kornberg</a>) after he discovered an enzyme that can synthesize RNA in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> However, the enzyme discovered by Ochoa (<a href="/wiki/Polynucleotide_phosphorylase" title="Polynucleotide phosphorylase">polynucleotide phosphorylase</a>) was later shown to be responsible for RNA degradation, not RNA synthesis. In 1956 Alex Rich and David Davies hybridized two separate strands of RNA to form the first crystal of RNA whose structure could be determined by X-ray crystallography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sequence of the 77 nucleotides of a yeast tRNA was found by <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Holley" title="Robert W. Holley">Robert W. Holley</a> in 1965,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> winning Holley the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="List of Nobel laureates in Physiology or Medicine">1968 Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> (shared with <a href="/wiki/Har_Gobind_Khorana" title="Har Gobind Khorana">Har Gobind Khorana</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshall_Nirenberg" title="Marshall Nirenberg">Marshall Nirenberg</a>).
</p><p>In the early 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">retroviruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reverse_transcriptase" title="Reverse transcriptase">reverse transcriptase</a> were discovered, showing for the first time that enzymes could copy RNA into DNA (the opposite of the usual route for transmission of genetic information). For this work, <a href="/wiki/David_Baltimore" title="David Baltimore">David Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renato_Dulbecco" title="Renato Dulbecco">Renato Dulbecco</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Howard_Temin" title="Howard Temin">Howard Temin</a> were awarded a Nobel Prize in 1975.
In 1976, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Fiers" title="Walter Fiers">Walter Fiers</a> and his team determined the first complete nucleotide sequence of an RNA virus genome, that of <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage_MS2" title="Bacteriophage MS2">bacteriophage MS2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1977, <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA_splicing" title="RNA splicing">RNA splicing</a> were discovered in both mammalian viruses and in cellular genes, resulting in a 1993 Nobel to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_A._Sharp" title="Philip A. Sharp">Philip Sharp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Roberts" title="Richard J. Roberts">Richard Roberts</a>.
Catalytic RNA molecules (<a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a>) were discovered in the early 1980s, leading to a 1989 Nobel award to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cech" title="Thomas Cech">Thomas Cech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Altman" title="Sidney Altman">Sidney Altman</a>. In 1990, it was found in <i><a href="/wiki/Petunia" title="Petunia">Petunia</a></i> that introduced genes can silence similar genes of the plant's own, now known to be a result of <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>At about the same time, 22 nt long RNAs, now called <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNAs</a>, were found to have a role in the <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">development</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">C. elegans</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
Studies on RNA interference gleaned a Nobel Prize for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_Z._Fire" title="Andrew Z. Fire">Andrew Fire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Craig_Mello" title="Craig Mello">Craig Mello</a> in 2006, and another Nobel was awarded for studies on the transcription of RNA to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Kornberg" title="Roger Kornberg">Roger Kornberg</a> in the same year. The discovery of gene regulatory RNAs has led to attempts to develop drugs made of RNA, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SiRNA" title="SiRNA">siRNA</a>, to silence genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Adding to the Nobel prizes awarded for research on RNA in 2009 it was awarded for the elucidation of the atomic structure of the ribosome to <a href="/wiki/Venki_Ramakrishnan" title="Venki Ramakrishnan">Venki Ramakrishnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Steitz" title="Thomas A. Steitz">Thomas A. Steitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ada_Yonath" title="Ada Yonath">Ada Yonath</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relevance_for_prebiotic_chemistry_and_abiogenesis">Relevance for prebiotic chemistry and abiogenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Relevance for prebiotic chemistry and abiogenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a> hypothesized that RNA might be catalytic and suggested that the earliest forms of life (self-replicating molecules) could have relied on RNA both to carry genetic information and to catalyze biochemical reactions—an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_world_hypothesis" title="RNA world hypothesis">RNA world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In May 2022, scientists reported that RNA was found to be formed spontaneously on prebiotic <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_glass" title="Volcanic glass">basalt lava glass</a> which is presumed to have been abundantly available on the <a href="/wiki/Early_Earth" title="Early Earth">early Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20220519_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20220519-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20220603_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20220603-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2015, complex <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and RNA <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>, were reportedly formed in the laboratory under <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> conditions, using starter chemicals, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compound</a> commonly found in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">Pyrimidine</a>, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs), is one of the most carbon-rich compounds found in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> and may have been formed in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a> and gas clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150303_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150303-87">[87]</a></sup> In July 2022, astronomers reported the discovery of massive amounts of <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis#Producing_molecules:_prebiotic_synthesis" title="Abiogenesis">prebiotic molecules</a>, including possible RNA precursors, in the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA-20220708_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-20220708-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FASS-20220708_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FASS-20220708-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
